# Architecture Map: Scientific Computing Data Flow

## Your Task

You are onboarding to a scientific computing team that uses the Python ML stack.
A senior engineer has asked you to produce a technical map of how data flows from
**raw array creation through scientific computation** across the three core libraries:
numpy, pandas, and scipy.

**Your question**: Map the data flow from raw array creation through scientific
computation across these repos. Your explanation must trace through all three layers:

1. **Array computation layer** — What function in `numpy/numpy` is the canonical
   entry point for array-level aggregation on raw ndarray objects?
2. **Data structure layer** — What class in `pandas-dev/pandas` wraps a NumPy ndarray
   as a pandas extension array, enabling interoperability between the two libraries?
3. **Scientific computation layer** — What function in `scipy/scipy` accepts numpy
   arrays (or pandas Series) as inputs for statistical analysis?

For each step, cite the specific repository, file path, and function/class name.

## Context

You are working with the Python ML stack in a cross-org environment:

- Local `/workspace/scikit-learn/` — `scikit-learn/scikit-learn` (ML algorithms)
- Accessible via Sourcegraph MCP:
  - `numpy/numpy` (array-computing)
  - `pandas-dev/pandas` (dataframe-library)
  - `scipy/scipy` (scientific-computing)

This question is specifically designed to benefit from cross-repo synthesis. The
data flow spans multiple organizations and can only be fully understood by examining
all three repos together.

## Output Format

Create a file at `/workspace/answer.json` with your findings:

```json
{
  "chain": [
    {
      "repo": "numpy/numpy",
      "path": "relative/path/to/file.py",
      "symbol": "FunctionOrClassName",
      "description": "What role this plays in the data flow"
    }
  ],
  "text": "Comprehensive narrative explaining how data flows from raw array creation through scientific computation, citing specific files and functions from each repo."
}
```

**Important**: Use exact repo identifiers as they appear in Sourcegraph. The oracle expects `repo` values of `numpy/numpy` (array layer), `pandas-dev/pandas` (data structure layer), and `scipy/scipy` (scientific computation layer). The `repo` field must match these exactly.
**Note**: Sourcegraph MCP tools return repo names with a `github.com/` prefix (e.g., `github.com/sg-benchmarks/kubernetes-client-go`). Strip this prefix in your answer — use `sg-benchmarks/kubernetes-client-go`, NOT `github.com/sg-benchmarks/kubernetes-client-go`.
**Provenance**: Your `text` narrative is evaluated for completeness. It must include repository names verbatim in `org/repo` format (e.g., `numpy/numpy`, `pandas-dev/pandas`, `scipy/scipy`) and file paths using slash notation (e.g., `numpy/_core/fromnumeric.py`), not Python module dot notation.

The `chain` should contain at least 3 steps representing the 3 layers described above.

## Evaluation

Your answer will be scored on:
- **Flow coverage**: Does the chain include key steps from all 3 layers (array creation → pandas integration → scipy computation)?
- **Technical accuracy**: Are the cited file paths and function/class names correct?
- **Provenance**: Does your narrative reference all three repositories with specific file paths?
- **Synthesis quality** (supplementary): Does the explanation connect the layers in a way that reveals the ecosystem architecture?

  2358→        has an integer dtype of less precision than the default platform
  2359→        integer.  In that case, if `a` is signed then the platform integer
  2360→        is used while if `a` is unsigned then an unsigned integer of the

31-assistanttool_useRead
32-usertool_resultRead
  3970→        \sqrt{\frac{\sum_i{|a_i - \bar{a}|^2 }}{N - 1}}
  3971→
  3972→    In statistics, the resulting quantity is sometimes called the "sample
  3973→    standard deviation" because if `a` is a random sample from a larger
  3974→    population, this calculation provides the square root of an unbiased
  3975→    estimate of the variance of the population. The use of :math:`N-1` in the
  3976→    denominator is often called "Bessel's correction" because it corrects for
  3977→    bias (toward lower values) in the variance estimate introduced when the
  3978→    sample mean of `a` is used in place of the true mean of the population.
  3979→    The resulting estimate of the standard deviation is still biased, but less
  3980→    than it would have been without the correction. For this quantity, use
  3981→    ``ddof=1``.
  3982→
  3983→    Note that, for complex numbers, `std` takes the absolute
  3984→    value before squaring, so that the result is always real and nonnegative.
  3985→
  3986→    For floating-point input, the standard deviation is computed using the same
  3987→    precision the input has. Depending on the input data, this can cause
  3988→    the results to be inaccurate, especially for float32 (see example below).
  3989→    Specifying a higher-accuracy accumulator using the `dtype` keyword can
  3990→    alleviate this issue.
  3991→
  3992→    Examples
  3993→    --------
  3994→    >>> import numpy as np

  3996→    >>> np.std(a)

  4170→    .. math::
  4171→
  4172→        \frac{\sum_i{|a_i - \bar{a}|^2}}{N - 1}
  4173→
  4174→    In statistics, the resulting quantity is sometimes called the "sample
  4175→    variance" because if `a` is a random sample from a larger population,
  4176→    this calculation provides an unbiased estimate of the variance of the
  4177→    population.  The use of :math:`N-1` in the denominator is often called
  4178→    "Bessel's correction" because it corrects for bias (toward lower values)
  4179→    in the variance estimate introduced when the sample mean of `a` is used
  4180→    in place of the true mean of the population. For this quantity, use
  4181→    ``ddof=1``.
  4182→
  4183→    Note that for complex numbers, the absolute value is taken before
  4184→    squaring, so that the result is always real and nonnegative.
  4185→
  4186→    For floating-point input, the variance is computed using the same
  4187→    precision the input has.  Depending on the input data, this can cause
  4188→    the results to be inaccurate, especially for `float32` (see example
  4189→    below).  Specifying a higher-accuracy accumulator using the ``dtype``
  4190→    keyword can alleviate this issue.
  4191→
  4192→    Examples
  4193→    --------
  4194→    >>> import numpy as np
  4195→    >>> a = np.array([[1, 2], [3, 4]])
  4196→    >>> np.var(a)
  4197→    1.25
  4198→    >>> np.var(a, axis=0)
  4199→    array([1.,  1.])
  4200→    >>> np.var(a, axis=1)
  4201→    array([0.25,  0.25])
  4202→
  4203→    In single precision, var() can be inaccurate:
  4204→
  4205→    >>> a = np.zeros((2, 512*512), dtype=np.float32)
  4206→    >>> a[0, :] = 1.0
  4207→    >>> a[1, :] = 0.1
  4208→    >>> np.var(a)
  4209→    np.float32(0.20250003)

def mean(a, axis=None, dtype=None, out=None, keepdims=np._NoValue, *, where=np._NoValue):
    kwargs = {}
    if keepdims is not np._NoValue:
        kwargs['keepdims'] = keepdims
    if where is not np._NoValue:
        kwargs['where'] = where

        try:
            mean = a.mean
        except AttributeError:
            pass
        else:
            return mean(axis=axis, dtype=dtype, out=out, **kwargs)

## Summary

I've successfully located and analyzed the classes in the pandas repository that wrap NumPy ndarrays as pandas extension arrays. Here are the key findings:

### Primary Class: **NumpyExtensionArray**

**Location:** `/workspace/pandas/pandas/core/arrays/numpy_.py` (563 lines)

**Key Characteristics:**
- This is the main class that directly wraps NumPy ndarrays as a pandas ExtensionArray
- Inherits from `OpsMixin`, `NDArrayBackedExtensionArray`, and `ObjectStringArrayMixin`
- Stores the wrapped ndarray in the `_ndarray` attribute
- Wraps 1-dimensional NumPy arrays only

**Core Features:**
1. **Constructor** (`__init__`): Takes a NumPy ndarray and wraps it, with optional copy parameter
2. **NumPy Integration**: Implements `__array__()` and `__array_ufunc__()` for NumPy interoperability
3. **Type**: Has `_typ = "npy_extension"` to identify itself internally
4. **Validation**: Raises `ValueError` if array is not 1-dimensional or not an ndarray

**Key Methods:**
- `_from_sequence()`: Class method to create from array-like objects
- `_from_backing_data()`: Creates new instance with different backing ndarray
- `astype()`: Type conversion support
- `to_numpy()`: Conversion back to raw ndarray with NA value handling
- Comprehensive reduction operations: `sum()`, `mean()`, `min()`, `max()`, `std()`, `var()`, `sem()`, `kurt()`, `skew()`, etc.
- Arithmetic and comparison operations through `_cmp_method()` and `_arith_method`

### Base Class Hierarchy: **NDArrayBackedExtensionArray**

**Location:** `/workspace/pandas/pandas/core/arrays/_mixins.py` (starts at line 92)

**Purpose:** Abstract base class for any ExtensionArray backed by a single NumPy ndarray

**Key Features:**

 10735→        else:
 10736→            prob = 0.0

 10738→        intercept_stderr = 0.0

 10741→        # n-2 degrees of freedom because 2 has been used up

   111→def _chk_asarray(a, axis, *, xp=None):
   112→    if xp is None:
   113→        xp = array_namespace(a)
   114→
   115→    if axis is None:
   116→        a = xp.reshape(a, (-1,))
   117→        outaxis = 0
   118→    else:
   119→        a = xp.asarray(a)
   120→        outaxis = axis
   121→
   122→    if a.ndim == 0:
   123→        a = xp.reshape(a, (-1,))
   124→
   125→    return a, outaxis
   126→
   127→
   128→def _chk2_asarray(a, b, axis):
   129→    if axis is None:
   130→        a = np.ravel(a)
   131→        b = np.ravel(b)
   132→        outaxis = 0
   133→    else:
   134→        a = np.asarray(a)
   135→        b = np.asarray(b)
   136→        outaxis = axis
   137→
   138→    if a.ndim == 0:
   139→        a = np.atleast_1d(a)
   140→    if b.ndim == 0:
   141→        b = np.atleast_1d(b)
   142→
   143→    return a, b, outaxis
   144→
   145→
   146→def _convert_common_float(*arrays, xp=None):
   147→    xp = array_namespace(*arrays) if xp is None else xp
   148→    arrays = [_asarray(array, subok=True) for array in arrays]
   149→    dtypes = [(xp.asarray(1.).dtype if xp.isdtype(array.dtype, 'integral')
   150→               else array.dtype) for array in arrays]
   151→    dtype = xp.result_type(*dtypes)
   152→    arrays = [xp.astype(array, dtype, copy=False) for array in arrays]
   153→    return arrays[0] if len(arrays)==1 else tuple(arrays)
   154→

### The Three-Layer Architecture:

1. **Array Computation Layer (numpy/numpy)**
   - **Entry Point**: `sum()` function in `numpy/_core/fromnumeric.py`
   - **Role**: Canonical API for array-level aggregation operations on raw ndarray objects
   - Delegates to optimized implementations in `numpy/_core/_methods.py`

2. **Data Structure Layer (pandas-dev/pandas)**
   - **Bridge Class**: `NumpyExtensionArray` in `pandas/core/arrays/numpy_.py`
   - **Role**: Wraps numpy arrays as pandas ExtensionArray objects, enabling seamless interoperability
   - Implements `__array_ufunc__()` and `__array__()` for NumPy function compatibility

3. **Scientific Computation Layer (scipy/scipy)**
   - **Canonical Function**: `describe()` in `scipy/stats/_stats_py.py`
   - **Role**: Accepts numpy arrays (and pandas Series via array interface) for comprehensive statistical analysis
   - Converts inputs to numpy arrays and performs multi-faceted statistical computations

### Key Integration Points:

- Data created in numpy remains as ndarray objects that flow directly into pandas ExtensionArray wrappers
- Pandas Series containing NumpyExtensionArray internally are accepted by scipy functions through the array interface
- No unnecessary data copying occurs—only wrapping and interface compliance
- All aggregation and statistical operations delegate back to numpy's optimized computation layer

Default is 'two-sided'.\n 10582\u2192        The following options are available:\n 10583\u2192\n 10584\u2192        * 'two-sided': the slope of the regression line is nonzero\n 10585\u2192        * 'less': the slope of the regression line is less than zero\n 10586\u2192        * 'greater':  the slope of the regression line is greater than zero\n 10587\u2192\n 10588\u2192        .. versionadded:: 1.7.0\n 10589\u2192\n 10590\u2192    Returns\n 10591\u2192    -------\n 10592\u2192    result : ``LinregressResult`` instance\n 10593\u2192        The return value is an object with the following attributes:\n 10594\u2192\n 10595\u2192        slope : float\n 10596\u2192            Slope of the regression line.\n 10597\u2192        intercept : float\n 10598\u2192            Intercept of the regression line.\n 10599\u2192        rvalue : float\n 10600\u2192            The Pearson correlation coefficient. To have access to all the computed values, including the\n 10631\u2192    standard error of the intercept, use the return value as an object\n 10632\u2192    with attributes, e.g.::\n 10633\u2192\n 10634\u2192        result = linregress(x, y)\n 10635\u2192        print(result.intercept, result.intercept_stderr)\n 10636\u2192\n 10637\u2192    Examples\n 10638\u2192    --------\n 10639\u2192    >>> import numpy as np\n 10640\u2192    >>> import matplotlib.pyplot as plt\n 10641\u2192    >>> from scipy import stats\n 10642\u2192    >>> rng = np.random.default_rng()\n 10643\u2192\n 10644\u2192    Generate some data:\n 10645\u2192\n 10646\u2192    >>> x = rng.random(10)\n 10647\u2192    >>> y = 1.6*x + rng.random(10)\n 10648\u2192\n 10649\u2192    Perform the linear regression:\n 10650\u2192\n 10651\u2192    >>> res = stats.linregress(x, y)\n 10652\u2192\n 10653\u2192    Coefficient of determination (R-squared):\n 10654\u2192\n 10655\u2192    >>> print(f\"R-squared: {res.rvalue**2:.6f}\")\n 10656\u2192    R-squared: 0.717533\n 10657\u2192\n 10658\u2192    Plot the data along with the fitted line:\n 10659\u2192\n 10660\u2192    >>> plt.plot(x, y, 'o', label='original data')\n 10661\u2192    >>> plt.plot(x, res.intercept + res.slope*x, 'r', label='fitted line')\n 10662\u2192    >>> plt.legend()\n 10663\u2192    >>> plt.show()\n 10664\u2192\n 10665\u2192    Calculate 95% confidence interval on slope and intercept:\n 10666\u2192\n 10667\u2192    >>> # Two-sided inverse Students t-distribution\n 10668\u2192    >>> # p - probability, df - degrees of freedom\n 10669\u2192    >>> from scipy.stats import t\n 10670\u2192    >>> tinv = lambda p, df: abs(t.ppf(p/2, df))\n 10671\u2192\n 10672\u2192    >>> ts = tinv(0.05, len(x)-2)\n 10673\u2192    >>> print(f\"slope (95%): {res.slope:.6f} +/- {ts*res.stderr:.6f}\")\n 10674\u2192    slope (95%): 1.453392 +/- 0.743465\n 10675\u2192    >>> print(f\"intercept (95%): {res.intercept:.6f}\"\n 10676\u2192    ...       f\" +/- {ts*res.intercept_stderr:.6f}\")\n 10677\u2192    intercept (95%): 0.616950 +/- 0.544475\n 10678\u2192\n 10679\u2192    \"\"\"\n 10680\u2192    TINY = 1.0e-20\n 10681\u2192    if y is None:  # The `sum` function at line 2339 in `numpy/_core/fromnumeric.py` accepts an ndarray and delegates to the backend implementation in `numpy/_core/_methods.py`. This delegation pattern isolates documentation and API design from performance-critical computation, allowing numpy to maintain both clarity and speed.\\n\\nThe aggregation functions in `numpy/_core/fromnumeric.py` handle subclass dispatch through the `_wrapreduction()` function, which checks if the input is a pure ndarray or a subclass that overrides aggregation methods.
